Oh, children, now that I have completed this book, which is the last of the science of medicine, for the completion of which I have labored to its end, and through its explanations and declarations, it is clear to me that I have composed this treatise for you, which pertains to my surgical operations.
For our operation of the hand is practiced in our region and in our times as almost entirely private, although it is a science greatly needed, and its practice has been obscured; and only some minor descriptions remain in the books of the Ancients, which they modified to correct errors and hesitations that have closed off understanding of it and weakened its force and art.
And it is evident to me that I should revitalize that part of this treatise in accordance with the way of explanation and declaration and abbreviation; and for the sake of adding the forms of the cautery tools and other instruments of the work, which serves as an addition to the declaration and preparation thereof, a necessary cause for this.
More the cause why a good craftsman does not apply his hand well in our time is that the art of medicine is long and that its practitioner must be trained in the science of anatomy which Galen knew well enough to understand the youth of the members and their forms, and the conjunction and separation of them; and had knowledge of the bones, the nerves, and the arteries, their number and their diseases; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and of their places.
And for this Hippocrates says that in name is little, but more in the work and especially in the art of the hand.
More we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for who does not know what we have said about anatomy will not be free from error due to which many men will die, as I have seen too many of them. For some are formed in this science and proceed from that same science and its experience.
For I saw a physician who, being foolish and ignorant, incurred an apostematia scropholosa in the neck of a woman, and injured some arteries so that great blood flowed out, causing the woman to die among convulsions.
And there was another doctor who almost drained a stone from a man who had suffered in his condition, and the stone was large, and brought it to him and dried that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I have already called for the extraction of that, and I see that due to the size of the stone and the position of the patient upon him, he will die.
And there was another doctor who took payment from a duke of our land for surgery, and he arrived at a blacksmith who had suffered a fracture in his thigh; and because of the doctor's ignorance, he extracted the fracture, over the wound, with pillows and splints of strong structure, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then the dislocation according to his desires, and then the rejoining, and then the letting it rest for several days, commanding us to loosen the bandage; and we stayed like this until the thigh and foot recovered, and proceeded to our detriment.
And I was called to him, and prepared to dislodge the stone, and peace and attempt to alleviate his pains was achieved.
But however, corruption was confirmed in the member, because I could not restrain that, and did not cease to walk the corruption in the member while the illness persisted.
And I saw another doctor who incised an oncotic abscess, and it became ulcerated over several days, during which the evil increased in it with the lord of it.
The cause is that the disease is purely of melancholic humor, and does not reach everywhere, but affects only the member that suffers from everything being hostile to it.
And for this reason, my children, it is necessary for you that the operation be devised in two divisions: one where the operation is associated with health, and another where the work is perilous in the major dispositions.
And I have already existed here in all parts of this book where the operation is filled with error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to guard this and leave that, so that the leaves do not find a way to speak and deceive you.
And therefore take your weapons with diligence and protection of God, and your patients with felicity and firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God seeking salvation and laudable success, and lay aside the terrible illnesses difficult to cure, and let your weapons be of what fears you, so that sitting in your faith and in your name you are not deceived: for more remains to your glory, and rather in this world and after in your sanctity.
Galia already has been dissected in some surgical texts: Do not treat illness falsely, so that bad doctors will not be named.
And you should now have dedicated this treatise according to three chapters.
Begin the division of the treatise, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered of the foot, concerning the forms of the instruments and the tools of the cautery, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on drying and puncturing, and on cups, and on blisters, and on the extraction of arrows and similar things, all categorized and ordered by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and on the cure of sprains and similar conditions; a chapter ordered by the head between the feet, and by the forms of the instruments.
Know what I will say about the quality of the coals of that cautery and its effects, and in which conditions the use of a cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the discourse on the quality of the cautery coals and its effects is long and prolix, subtle by science, and hidden in secret; much has already been said about it by wise men, and they are divided in their opinions. It suffices me to touch on a little of their sermons, fearing length.
And therefore say that a cautery is applied to all compositions universally which are of flesh or sinew, except two compositions, which are compositions of sinew.
In the sinewy and dry composition, the flesh will desiccate; for some said that a cautery is beneficial for that, while others said the opposite, that a cautery is not appropriate in malaise which is due to quality and dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inappropriate that such malaise, being sinewy and dry, be healed by that which is cautery and dry.
And say that those who say the opposite, that by cautery one is cured in all sinewy and dry malaise that comes in men's bodies, for when you compare man's body and its humors to the composition of fire, you find man's body humid.
And I say according to the saying of that, which I have already experienced many times myself.
But I do not consider that it comes to those who have been extensively trained in the Capitol of Cauterization by long practice, and who know the diversity of human composition and the disposition of maladies in their varieties, and the causes of those, and the accidents related to them, as well as the duration of those.
In other complications, it is not about fear, and especially in wet diseases, for all doctors agree on those, and are not divided in using cautery in them.
Understand, sons, that the secret of curing by cautery with fire and its goodness lies in the burning medicine, for fire is simple, its action reaching only the member being cauterezed and not affecting other members much except with slight harm.
But the action of the burning medicine reaches that member from which it is distant, and strongly causes in the affected member a disease difficult to heal and quickly makes it worse.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not cause anything other than to superfluously consume. And we are already familiar with this through experience and long service in studying the art concerning the truth of causes; and for this reason, I excuse myself for the length of my discourse. If it were not that this is not written at liberty in this pause, and that you secretly hide your knowledge about fire, and the quality of its operation on bodies and its expulsion through which you expel diseases, then this discourse would be a philosophical demonstration, which would benefit your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time at which it is convenient to make a cautery and when the best season is spring.
And I say that a cautery is convenient at all times, because the damage from the accidental season is submerged in the youth of what is burnt by the cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, strong and fistulas, which do not suffer delay because it is feared of those that come evil intentions, which are greater than minor damage entering through the season.
And thus they do not place a cautery on your feet, or, sons, they only consider the common people and the unskilled physicians, for they believe that the ailment that is cured by a cautery is not effective at all times, and they avoid such a consequence.
And the reason is not that they care for this, for a cautery is nothing more in medicine than a means to alter the composition and dry the humors which are the cause of the venous pains.
But nonetheless, a cautery can be superfluous in medicine for the sake of the speed of its efficacy and the virtue of its operation and the greatness of its lord.
It is possible that their illness returns at some point in time, according to the complexity of the disease and the location of their illness, as well as their virtue, and what is prepared in their body through the aggregation of superfluities within them, and what is acquired by them in food and semblance of those causes; but the illness in which a cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a limb with few superfluities and humidities, just as a tooth cautery for pain and similar to that, it is possible that such pain does not return, more so when this is done in small amounts.
It is more common to say that a cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a surgeon's saying, not what I understand by it, for I believe it is not a cure that benefits either medicine or any other cause except the cautery.
And the cause is contrary to this; and I understand that a cautery is the last remedy of medicine only when one administers the methods of cure in some illnesses, and those medicines do not work, and near the end of the treatment, one administers a cautery, which then benefits that condition. And at this point, the last remedy of medicine is the cautery, not according to the understanding which I understand the vulgar and too many of the foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that a cautery made of gold is better than one made of iron, and they did not say this only because of the equality of gold and the subtlety of its substance.
And they said that the place for a cautery does not burn, since it is not absolutely heated, for I have already tested this and found that it does not heat all bodies equally, and a gold cautery is good and better than one made of iron as they say; but when you make a cautery with fire on gold, it is indeed so, for the gold ignites according to the quantity you want due to the corrosion of the gold, and prepares to receive fire; and if you adjust over this ignition, the gold melts by the fire, and settles in the fire with some sheets so that the craftsman can cut through its occupation.
And for this, when making a cautery of iron, you should use it more lightly and more properly according to the correctness of the operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter concerning the cautery according to other chapters, and I have ordered those of the foot within, so that I delay the one requesting what they want from me.
The first chapter.
This cautery treats cold and humidity over the servant accident of which there are two causes, causing soda and much traumatic flux, called corissa, from the head to the parts of the hollows and the ears flowing, and much drowsiness, and tooth pain, and the gullet, and universally all illness that comes through coldness, thus as paralytically, soda and apoplexy, and similarities in those illnesses.
The form of this cautery is as follows: first, I command the patient to be evacuated with a laxative medicine on an empty stomach for three or four nights, according to the power of the illness and the age of the patient and their habits.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and place that razor between your hands on a leaning bench so that he places his hands on his own foot.
And then pause the razor from your palm near his mouth between his palms. More over, since the correct location has been determined, mark that location with a probe, and then heat the olive cautery of which this is the form:
And then place that cautery over the marked location with a probe, in such a way that it descends gradually, and your hand is gradually constrained, and you rotate it.
And then lift your hand slightly and look at the location. If you see any part of the bone being revealed through the flesh, as much as an err (a small amount), then lift your hand. If not, return your hand to that iron or another, if it is cooled, so that what I have said about it can be seen on the bone.
And then take a little of salt, dissolve it in that [solution], and leave it there for three days.
And then place it on the wound in the groin and leave it there until the scar of the burn is destroyed. And then treat the area with an ointment of tetrafarmakon until it is healed.
And they also say that when the wound is opened wide, it heals better, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the knee should be cauterized inside the bone, and that the cautery should remain until part of the bone tissue is burnt.
And then scrape what is attached to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that the cautery should reach the marrow, pressing firmly on the bone until the bone cools, and this is the method of Alphécat: And they estimate from that place exhale the vapors of the head.
And let the wound be kept open for a long time, and then cure it before it becomes consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery for all, except in some men according to a way of fear; and leave that one, my advice is, it is better to be healed when possible; because the head is developed by solution of continuity of the skull as we have testified in other members, and especially when the head of the patient is of foul nature.
And the first type of cautery is more safe and better, my advice; use that one and do that work for healing.
Capitulum secundum.
On the cautery for the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs in the head, and this has been prolonged in the patient, and they use various treatments such as powders, seeds, cap purge, plasters, poultices, cats, bandages, and similar remedies, and especially if the head has already been cauterized with a cautery which does not improve the condition for any of these causes, consider whether the head of the patient is of strong constitution in its nature; and there is no foul or great cold, then this cautery should be reconsidered. Leave that one for another cautery and use it only slightly. And then cauterize that on the top of his head between where the vein is divided. Incise the vein and the quantity of the opening as we have counted, and cauterize that with a cautery at the back of the head in the place where it is called vas, — and this is the place where suction is applied. And lift your hand here, and do not uncover the head, for the patient finds great pain from this different from the pain of all other head cauterizations.
And I will say this cauterization in its place, for know that the cauterization with which one cauterizes the horns of the head and the occiput is a finer cauterization than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Head complete.
Of the cauterization for new migraine.
When there is a sharp pain in the back of the head, or a prolonged pain in the temples, medicinals should be applied to the head to relieve it, and another treatment, which I have described for diseases, should be administered. If this does not help, then cauterization can be performed in two ways: with a burning acute medicinal substance, or with iron.
May a cautery that burns in medicine be that you take a piece of aloes, and soak it there and bind the extremities of it from both ends. And then apply the spot of pain on the temple with a lancet laid gently there and make the cut where it is to be hidden.
And then strengthen the place where it is stopped by descent compression, and leave it for the space of three hours; and then remove and cut the aloes, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then cover over the spot submerge in balsam until the area heals.
And then treat that wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, use those wounds which some medicines burn, the ones I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
May the cautery be performed in this manner: heat it with fire, of which this is the form:
and it is called a key lock, because the head of it has the form of a clove and in the middle location there is a small projection.

And if the amount that is burned due to the strength of the cauterization is as much as its size, lift your hand so that the artery which is left is not burned, so that there be no flow of blood.
Then immerse it in salt water and place it over the area, leaving it for three days. Then place it near a warm place, and apply ointment until it is healed.
And if you do, cauterize at the tip of the knife edge according to the cautery, and guard against cutting the artery in this new migraine.

Chapter V.
On the cauterization of ear pains.
When there is pain in the ear due to cold and it is treated with hot applications, and another treatment as I have said in the division does not relieve the pain, then apply the cautery, which is called puncta. This is its form:
And then puncture near the beginning, and extend it to the vicinity of the whole ear as if a wheel is turned, or in the circuit of each place if the pain is in any part. Lengthen the cautery from the rim of the ear gradually placing it where you have tested the location.
And if the cautery with ten points around the entire ear, and cool it, then quickly cure the places between as they heal.
Capitol. vi.
Of the cautery for the tortura.
Tortura which is cured and treated with cautery is none other than due to the species that the vein carries, as we have said in the divisions of ailments.
Avoid cautery for the species that flows due to dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you cure that species of tortura with jerras, capipurgi, and guarismes, and your cure does not agree, then when you cauterize the maladie, you should make three cauteries: the first on the rim of the ear, the second a small one in its temple, and the third on the junction of two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected area, for softening effect occurs only in the part that is healthy.
The method of cautery is to apply the cautery on the outer side of the ear near the tip of the ear, placing a small piece of cloth over the top of the head. On the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the affected part, and then place your hand with the cautery within until the quantity of the material of the codena is burned.
This is the method of the cautery, and it is specific to the barber-surgeon, whose form is given below. However, more subtle is that you should add just a little as you see fit; and cut away a small amount to avoid unnecessary crudity.
Then cover the area with what is given and proceed to treat it until it is healed.

Of cauterization for oblivion through flame.

Capitol xi.
Of the cautery for melancholy.

Then place your hand on the patient's head, and properly make a torcortura (a circular incision) on it above the middle of the head.
And the patient should be bathed and kept from all sides.

And then place that cauterized area on the edge or in the folds of cloth.

Chapter XVI.
When the skin is converted to the inner parts and hand of the eyelid, use a cautery.
When the skin of the eyelid is converted to the inner parts, I must probe the eyelid. The cautery in such cases should be used according to two methods: either by applying a hot cautery or by using a burning medicine.
If the cautery is hot, command the patient to look away from where the skin is burned as if they were gazing at those who clean their skin, keeping it prolonged and equal. And when probing, you should grasp its root; then strengthen the eyelids with a bandage so that it does not stick until the skin has grown back.
Then, when they have grown and equalized, place the head of the patient's probe in their temple, and then press over the eyelid of the affected side, marking it with a mark shaped like the leaf of a laurel, which will be the beginning of the mark relative to the skin.
And then place something hot in a bag or on a poultice over the affected area.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the figure which is marked, little by little, in stages, while the surface of the wound, which is like a bay leaf, is being burned entirely appropriately.
And a sign of recovery from your work is that you see the eyelid of the affected area already contracted and the skin around it elevated due to the puncture of the wound.
And then lift your hand and leave it there for three days, and then place it over the wound between the burns to allow the scar to heal. And then apply ointment until it is healed.
But if for some cause the skin returns to the area near its origin and the eyelid becomes softened, then make another pass with the cautery over that area as you did at first.
But if the skin is on the eyelid, then cauterize it there where it is elevated to its natural place and make it equal so that the skin does not remain uneven.
But the cautery for medical burning commands the patient to extend the skin where it is to be made equal, and then make the shape of a mulberry leaf from linen cloth.
And then take a known soap and fresh fat, one pound or as much as is needed, and mix these fats well, preparing it without rubbing; then spread this mixture over the linen cloth which is made thus in the shape of a mulberry leaf, and place that on the skin or both; place the remaining part cut into small pieces in honey, and position the patient's head at the center.
And place that on the medicine and press it slightly so that the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like that of fire.
And thus, when you observe that the inflammation is biting, then remove the medicine and press it at the center; as soon as the biting ceases, replace the medicine and wash the area with water; then consider if the eyelid has already been lifted, and if so, apply heat or incision, for then it is good; if not, return to applying the medicine in the place where it did not bite, ensuring that your operation remains equal and contracts the area.
And then place your hand over the groin area between which the root of the adhesion is to be removed, and cure that with an ointment or something else in which it is healed.
And you must, with due diligence, ensure that no error in the medicine occurs during your operation. If the eyelid becomes softened or some cause of skin swelling appears on it within a few days, then repeat your medication on that place where the eyelid has become softened, and proceed in this manner as you did at the beginning, and soon let it heal completely.
This is the way to treat a cataract.
And know that the eyelids of humans are diverse in size and shape, and therefore your incision should be made according to this.
And the way of making the incision should not be hidden from him who studies this art.
Chapter XVII.
On the cautery treatment of a fistula in the lacrimal duct of the eyelid.
When you treat a fistula as we have said in the divisions of the ailments and do not apply your treatment, then when you cauterize it according to this manner: command the patient to place his head on your thigh, and let another minister hold the head of the patient between your knees in such a way that it is neither hurt nor agitated.
And then place the hot iron on the flesh in the thigh or over the swelling of the boil above the hip.
And then heat the cautery, and this is its form:
which should be cylindrical, as is the form of a cane or a bird's feather quill, from one end of which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, let it penetrate into the other end. And if you want, let it be drawn out like a needle.
Yet, however, that which is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is opened, and draw from it clean and dry that which comes out. And if it is not opened, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place the well-ignited cautery over it, and hold your hand a little below the root of the nose to prevent your hand or the patient from moving, and then place the cautery on the cheeks and destroy that area.
And so, if at the beginning of your cautery it goes into the mouth, that is good; otherwise, then reapply the cautery one or two more times if necessary; and leave it like this for three days, and then place a border around the area and apply an ointment that is dry until it heals.

Book VII.
On the treatment of ear abscesses.
Sometimes children are born with abscesses in the flesh of their ears from their parents' unperforated ears, and this happens occasionally in some people, with an abscess forming in the ear肉 here due to infection or no apparent cause; sometimes these abscesses occur in the cartilage of the ear before it fully develops, and sometimes they appear on the outer parts.
And that which occurs in the anterior part of it, which does not affect the surface, is in the major dispositions of difficult recovery.
And in that where you can see the surface, make sure that you place the patient in the sun and examine it. And if you see an apparent swelling, open that thin membrane under which this swelling is located:
If the extremity of which is narrow in width, and one part is sharp while the other is not; and the membrane is two fingerbreadths wide so as not to irritate the ear.
And if the opacity is due to hidden flesh in the ear or in the肉中, then you should use thin scissors and cut through it with great delicacy so that all the flesh is completely removed.
And if the opacity is in the anterior part of the ear, then take a thin needle and heat that fire a little, and then place it in the flesh of the ear.
And so if you sense an accumulation of that which the patient experiences with ease due to the weight of excretion, relieve the oppression with ease; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
And then place the cloth in the ear according to the size of the wound which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or that you soak some and insert it into water, powder it thoroughly, and use it; and if you see the ear already swollen, then change the liquid and substitute another that you wrap in lint made from rubbed oil, until the swelling is reduced; and cure it as it heals.
But if a flow of blood occurs, then immerse the sponge or cloth in cold water, and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which you tighten to restrict the flow of blood, which is called in the treatise on distillation.

Capitol.xl.
In which the main discourse on boils and their treatment is presented.
Boils are special lumps, of various types, according to the memory of them, of each one in this treatise.
And they vary in their perforation and treatment through two methods. Of these, one is specific to the type of boils themselves; and it concerns what is grasped from the humors.
And the second method is specific to the locations where bodily accidents occur, for a boil that occurs on the head is different from one that occurs in the anus; and a boil that arises in a fleshly location is different from one that arises at a joint; and for each of them, their judgment is made through an operation.
And more of abscesses some of which cover that they not be perforated but near maturation of the health in those or complement of their.
So other abscesses some of which cover that they be lanced when crossed, and are not matured according to their complement, thus as abscesses that are near joints, for an abscess when near a joint, the nature of it is prolonged into where·s poyreys you that is in the circuit of it, and strongly let the veins or nerves of that joint be corrupted, for it may cause contraction of that member; or it is near the main limb, and if you delay the perforation of it until its maturity affects the main limb; or it is near the anus, therefore lance that which is not matured; and should not lance it crude, from lack of digestion, but one that is near the anus, so that it does not procure a prolapse of it and be elongated to the inner parts of the anus, and a fistula may be made, or that it be done in the term of it that is not healed. And you should know the time for lancing the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is the cessation of pain and the end of fever, and the decrease of swelling and pulse, and the subsidence of the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And know that when you can feel the perforation cage in the place just below the abscess and of pus coming from the cure of the prominence.
And the perforation should be made along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the thigh or feet, and at the location of the lacertae, of the nerve cords, of the arteries; and, accordingly, in all similar places where there is no reflection. For in those that are reflective, use perforation according to that member.
When the abscess is in a fleshly location, it is better to delay the perforation until the digestion of the pus is confirmed according to our previous saying; for if you perforate before that, it will prolong the course of the putrefaction and harden the walls of the abscess and increase its depth.
More abscesses should be punctured according to the width of the flesh or body as the need demands, or according to that from which the limb suffers.
And ensure that you administer a small puncture in large abscesses, and a large and broad or multiple sections according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And in other abscesses, ensure that the cord is returned to its cavity and tied, when this is done, do so while it is still warm, just as you would do with cords from piles and buboes.
And of those that are incised with a section having three angles; and in which an incision is made as the figure of a mulberry leaf, as with abscesses of boils.
And of these, there are those in which a circular section and a crescent section are administered, and similar to those.
More so for those where nothing is done in this manner, as with superficial abscesses, you should puncture them with a superficial or flat puncture only.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected some healthy pus, you should puncture it so that all the foul matter is not extracted at once; having extracted part of it, then on another day fill the abscess with more, and continue extracting larger parts in successive days until it is thoroughly drained.
And especially if the patient is of virile virtue, or a woman in labor, or a small child, or an old person in decrepitude, for the animal spirit resolved with the exit of the healthy pus weakens the malady, and you must open it.
Take great care with this chapter, and when you puncture the abscess in which you are going to make your incision, ensure that you cleanse the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small, or a flat section, in which case you should apply some locally effective remedies; if the abscess is large, and the openings from the puncture are large, then you must place your hand between each one until they heal together.
And if in the abscess you have divided part of the strand or concaved it in the circuit, then you must complete it with fine linen or a thread made of linen cloth, just as when you have drawn out bandages or roots, to maintain their moisture; strengthen it on the seventh day; and proceed with it and cure it as what is appropriate from the ointments until it is healed.
But if you happen to experience bleeding during the time of your operation, then administer water infused with wine in which you soak a cloth, and place that over the location of the bleeding several times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer treatment and cure with powders which I have recommended in several places of this our livre and division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is near many nerves, then you must infuse powders with wine and oil, and place those over the locations.
And if it is in the summer, and the locations are fleshy, then you must place powders infused with water or oil or with wine and oil, all of which should be applied on the third day, as we have said, and then you must drain the abscess, cleanse it, and administer to its cure what is appropriate for it until it is healed.

Chapter II.
On the treatment of an umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia is caused by various factors; it can be due to cracks in the diaphragm which is above the belly, and protrudes through the abdominal wall according to where other ruptures occur; or due to a tumor that flows from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or due to constricted fatty tissue.

And when you press on that area with your thumb, the swelling diminishes and quickly returns; it is light in that area, and a strong motive for treatment should involve bathing and vigorous work.
If it is due to humidity, then the pain is like that of a lion and does not diminish when you press on it with your hand; it is neither constricted nor relieved.
And if it is due to a wound, then in this case the abscess appears blackish in color.
If it is due to no flesh at all, then the abscess is heavy, large, and hard, and is located according to a certain quantity.
If it is due to excessiveness, then the touch of it is like that of a lion.
And the operation in such cases is that you consider whether the prominence of the navel is due to an artery, or a vein, or from phlegm; then you must refrain from treating it, for there is fear and dread as you have been taught in the Capitol where I said about abscesses from arteries and veins.

And if you have found the membrane, then take it with forceps; and incise its superfluous parts.

And the first ones said that it should be done this way as we have recounted in the hernia case; which is that you tie the vessels near the section above that facilitation of it; and then dry out in the middle place around the apostema with what I operate there, before the vessels fall.
And cure the plague as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter LXVII.
Of the disorder that occurs in the ankles.
In some cases, there is a rupture in the ankles as we have said, and you should treat the area.
And the genii do not attribute any cause of the intestines to the genii; and if they do, it is few; and return them to their place in all cases.
But if the time at that point is prolonged, there is an adjusted fissure in the chiasm where we insert the intestine in the chiasm; and this occurs due to the extension of the chiasm, which is in the ankles as we have said. The cause is that the chiasm is extended, and nearby it becomes softened, and the area becomes apostematous and you should treat it.
And the treatment of it is by cautery as it is preceded by the narration of it.
Sometimes it is treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on his side between your legs, and then the area of the rupture of the abscesses is dried out by heat according to the width as much as three fingers.
And then strengthen the white cloth that is under when it is uncovered.
And then take the rod and place it over the prominent area of the cloth, and press it against the front of the abdomen.
And then fix the two prominent areas over the ends of the rod of the cloth, and adjust one to the other with the stitch.
And then withdraw the end of the rod, and do not extend it completely out of the cloth, nor lengthen the stitch, nor any other cause as I know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat that in the treatment of wounds, and after incising the flaps, clean it and restore the wound before it is consolidated; for the cloth contracts and does not adjust the prominence of the rupture.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is more proximate to health.
Chapter LXXXVIII.
On the softening of the scrotum.
When spots appear on the scrotum in some men, it is softened because the flesh between those spots is softened, and attention should be paid to this.
Then it is necessary for the one who is consulted in the treatment of this to have the patient lie on their side, and completely expose the scrotum which is softened over the natural scrotum; and then draw the two lips together at the seam.
And if you wish, stitch in the beginning the excess of the softened scrotum with three or four stitches and a firm seam.
And then incise between the seams; and thereafter treat for the other wounds until it heals and the sutures fall out.
Capitol lxix.
Of castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but by cause it is allowed to me to remember it in this free treatise; and the remembrance of it is necessary for two ways, of which one is so that when a doctor is asked about it, he knows the cure for whom it happens; and the other way is that we might see there is a necessity for castration of other beasts for our young ones, such as from bulls, and from boars, and from pigs, and similar to beasts.
And therefore say that castration is done in two ways: either by excision, or by incision.
But in that which is done by excision, the way of the operation is to tie the beast's tail in water until the testicles are softened and relaxed. And then take those with your hand until they are soft and do not appear to have lumps when touched.
In castration performed by fissure and section, make sure you have the beast and press the cord of the testicles with your left hand.
Then lift the suspensory ligament, and incise over each groove between two processes.
Then incise those pus that you have from each excoriation. And do not leave on those of the scrotum any cause, but the thin skin which is over the veins.
And this method of castration is better which is performed by traction, for traction firmly leaves no cause of the testicles because the beast desires copulation.
Then cure the wound while it is healing.
Chapter 70.
On the cure of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in males occurs according to two methods: one where it appears that the space in the cord of testicles is opened, in which there is a mixture between two testes with some female characteristics in them.
And sometimes urine flows from that vein which is in the root of the testicles.
In women, it is special: and the vein in the woman's groin is like two testicles of a man, slightly larger, with some heaviness at the outer part, similar to the scrotum of a man and the two testicles.
And the cure is of three kinds: two for men and one for women. And it is that you should incise those tight tissues where the excess of that humor escapes, and then proceed to cure those with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second kind for men from which urine flows from the vein of the testicles, neither cure nor operation is needed.
Chapter 21.
On the enlargement and prominence of flesh in women due to certain causes.
Enlargement of the flesh is usually adjusted over natural causes where it is stretched and made firm by looking; sometimes in certain women it is magnified to such an extent that it extends like in men, and swells during intercourse.
Then you should have the excess mucus in your hand or cupped and drain it.
And do not finish in cupping, especially on the right side, lest there be a flow of blood.
And then care for the wound in its care and healing.
More hidden flesh is flesh that arises in the horror of the mother before it fills that space, and a strong cord is fixed to the parts outside according to the likeness of a vein (and for this some first called this "caudata malady"); therefore, we should cut it thus as we cut mucus, and care for it until it is healed.
Capitol. lxxii.
Of the care of alratica.
Alratica is when the canal of the female is not perforated, or is slightly injured. And it can be natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a malady which has preceded, and that one is caused by meat not fitting well or by a pulse thin or thick.
It is caused in the liver, or in the flanks of the body, or in the upper parts thereof, or in the lower parts thereof, and you prohibit coitus and conception and childbirth, and strongly you prohibit menstruation.
The knowledge of that is made by looking at the midwife, if the obstruction is apparent, probe gently; but if it is not apparent, then you examine that among the thighs or on the abdomen.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin pulse, and is close to the two lips [vagina], then you insert and dilate that.
And you place powders on the two lips [vagina], and then place your fingers indicating both sides near the entrance.
And the woman is on her back, her thighs extended, and then spread the two lips with force until that thin pulse is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool and soak it in oil and place it over the area, and leave a man there to prevent the incised place from becoming consolidated.
And if the ulcer is large and deep, then incise it with a spatula or leaf of mint.
And if the ulcer is superficial, then suspend it with a pin, and incise it there.
And use those medicines that stop the flow of venom from bites, such as aconite, dragon's blood, and ensese, prepared with alum.
And then administer a broad lead cannula to prevent the ulcer from becoming slightly consolidated.
And keep it there for several days and administer cooled wine.
And then treat with the other consolidation remedy before it is healed.
And sometimes accidents occur in the natural abundance of other tissues, then you should incise according to this method. If this is due to a painful abscess, for an abscess that is in the greater part, do not perform any opposition by iron in any manner.

Chapter 75.
To teach midwives how to treat miscarriages properly when they occur according to an unnatural figure.

Then when you see those signs, at once let the abdomen be compressed so that the fetus descends slightly; when it is on its head, let the second stage begin, and it is prepared for those excessive fluids through good management.
That which is another to this part is unnatural and unacceptable, for when the fetus is situated on its feet and the body of the mother is situated in front of the head of the fetus and its feet, or one hand, or at the side of the fetus, or the head of the fetus and one hand, or over the abdomen of the fetus, and heavily it is turned over its own back.

More when it is situated between the thighs, then command him to express it, and make that turn outward with a cloth, for it will be left hanging if God wills.

Of the cure of fistulas that develop wounds.
The fistulas that develop wounds with pus beneath are nodules and swellings which often occur near the buttocks or in the space of one side.
And it is a fistula or spots.
And when that nodule is old and flows with watery white or subtle rot, sometimes some of these fistulas will penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by the discharge and by the wind condition; sometimes the patient uses the opening, and strongly smells like those with worms.
And of these fistulas, sometimes in the space they penetrate into the bladder, and into the flesh of the groin; and of those that penetrate to the joint of the thigh and to the notch of the buttocks.
And in what is known of a fistula penetrating into the anus and non-penetrating, you should insert your finger into the anus, and introduce a thin thread or wire into the fistula when there is no pus.
And if there is pus in that area, then introduce a thin lead thread or horsehair thread into it, where you feel the thread or hair with your finger.
And if you do not feel that at all, nor any cause of evacuation, or worm, or flatulence as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and proceed to its cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the flesh of the groin, its significance is dribbling urine from there and prohibition thereof until the area with medicine is consolidated.
And if it penetrates into the junction of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the circuit, its sign is that the thread passes through here if there is no pus in the bone, and pain in the circuit of the limb; and that redness persists over time; and that it does not respond to topical medication, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those fistulas penetrating them, there is no cure for it, and the cure of those is hard work and frivolity of the leaf doctors over which they are bound.
Moreover in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing by iron is hoped for according to what I will recount to you, and what already I have done and attempted.
And it is that the patient lies on his back and elevates his legs between his thighs, the hips inclined towards the abdomen, and then insert a lead or iron probe into the fistula if it is tortuous, where you can feel the location where the probe passes.
And if that location is near the throat, then you must insert the probe at that point in the throat.
And if you feel in the probe itself the passage already clearly revealed, or anything between the probe and the siphac or flesh, then you can certainly determine that it is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
Some may say that it is healthy in some men to have gout.

Of the treatment of wounds.
And I have already said in the division of the treatment of wounds what is suitable for the free and the bound, and now I speak of the treatment of those who are more severe and require medicines.
Therefore, it is said that wounds are diversified according to what they are caused by and according to the place where the plaques occur.
And the causes why wounds are made are thus: by a stone impact, or a slash with a sword or dagger, or a blow with a lance, or a club, or an arrow, or similar to those causes of impacts.
More specifically, the wounds that occur in parts of the body are thus: a wound that occurs on the head, or the neck, or the foot, or the abdomen, or the thigh, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I hereby recommend the treatment of some wounds, as you will apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head ailments, for the recall of compound conditions will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
So say that when an injury to the head occurs and it is a simple headache and not a fracture, then consider: if it is due to a stone or something similar, and has caused swelling so much as to be a large bruise and you fear the sudden onset of an abscess in that area, then you should incise and perform bloodletting on the cephalic vein immediately, and do not delay this; and draw out from it as much blood as the strength of it allows, unless some reason prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood drawn from the bruise is limited.
And the bloodletting should be on the opposite side of the injured area; and one must guard against swelling and against heavy food and drink.
But if some reason prevents reduction, then diminish the food and do not approach meat or drink.
And place your hand over the bruise, if an abscess has formed, applying oil rubbed with rosemary or in wine that contains astringents.
But if it is certain that an abscess will form, then place yourself over the wound, if it is recent with its own secretion and the air has not changed there spread the powder which I will describe to you shortly.
But if the air has already changed there, then place yourself over some ointments until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or due to a rupture and the edges do not close properly with stitches, then adjust those with sutures according to the method of suturing that I will describe.
But if the wound is already open to bone and flesh, and it is not retention but only a small suspensory ligament, then cut into that and soon cure the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in its place. Command the patient to eat foods in which he takes pleasure: porridge and vegetables similar to them, until he recovers.
And if you find putrefaction in the flesh and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut out all of that and remove it, and soon cure it.
If in these plaques the artery or vein from which the flux of blood can occur is present and cannot be treated by medicines, then you inquire about the artery. If you find that uncut one, then cut it with a blade or ligate it; if necessity prompts you that what we have said does not apply, then cauterize where the blood flow has been stopped.
If these plaques are small and few, the cause is light; sprinkle this powder over them, for before the air changes, it will consolidate those.
The manner of the powder is this: take oil of enses divided one part, blood of dragon divided two parts, and caustic divided three parts; mix all these in equal parts and sift them, then fill the plaques with this mixture, confine it well by a good binding over that to which it adheres, and coagulate it with blood.
Then strengthen it above with powder and leave it, and do not remove it while it is not coagulated, for the medicine will not be effective until it is healed.
And sometimes this is done alone with a cautery, when it is not possible to see or touch the wound.
And this operation is performed on large wounds when you determine that they should be paused over those and stretched, and on recent wounds whose blood has not been altered by the air.

Of the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that any boil or apostema when wrapped and allowed to mature, and forms an ulcer, but is not consolidated and keeps growing over time without being incised, in whatever part of the body it is, universally it is named a fistula and we call it a abscess.
And according to truth, a fistula is a festered node, hard and white, upon which there is no pain and which is enclosed just as an abscess is. Therefore, it is called a white ulcer, that is, a painless abscess.
And in an abscess there is moisture, which keeps growing; and if the moisture is strong, it can be incised within a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, small, large, and thin.
All those causes have been shown, and the signs of them and the cure for them by medicine in this division.
And cover thus so that you may know the cure for fistula which is caused by surgical operation with the hand.

From the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to the species, and according to the places of the body in which they lodge, for their diversity is that some have large points, small, concave, and dull, being solid; and some have three angles, and of those to which belong tongues; and of those to which belong feathers.
And of the signs of suffocation is when the arrow lodges in it, pierces the bone, and sits above the skull, which is over the brain, for it happens by strong acute pain, scotoma, vertigo, redness in the palms, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of understanding, and vomiting of bile, and the speech is greatly impaired, the voice destroyed, and sitting at the site of the wound a white moisture resembling dust, and from it seeming like an excess of flesh.
And if you see these signs in this manner, then abstain from the cure and extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
And the signs of the arrow when it has pierced the heart are that it is near the left nipple, and felt at that point where it was lodged in a hard substance, not visible; and the strong movement of the arrow seems like the pulse, and the flow of the black and foul blood, and the coldness at the extremities, pallor of the face, and syncope; and from this you would know that death descends suddenly.
And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lung are spitting of bloody foam from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are pulsating, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily or irregularly, and demands air with difficulty.
And if the arrow pierces the periosteum which is in the foot, and the pain then is near the small bones, and makes the swelling great with a strong and spasmodic pain, and does not affect all the members of the muscles.
And if the arrow hits the stomach, then firmly press the wound to stop any indigestion.
And if the arrow hits the belly and is embedded in it, apply firm pressure to the wound or the skin, or the intestine which is ruptured, as there is no hope for the cure without removing the arrow.
And if the arrow hits the bladder and pierces through causing urine to leak out, and any process of it reaches the parts outside, then intense pain in the wound indicates that it is fatal.
Moreover, the other members such as the face, neck, shoulder, arm, and ribs, the vertebrae of the back, the sternum, the pelvis, and their counterparts in the limbs, are saved according to major dispositions when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a poisonous arrow.
And I will recount to you what I have seen about the nature of these arrows, so that you may understand it regarding their treatment.
The cause is that in a man an arrow struck the lacrimal gland of his eye in the root of his nose, and I extracted it from one side, left his ear gray, and he recovered, but nothing happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which struck him in the gray of his eye, leaving the eyelid behind, and the arrow was already hidden so that only a small extremity adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow among the sharp arrows of the Turks, embedded in iron where there were no ears.
And the Jew is alive, and nothing happened to his eye, which was badly injured.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which was lodged in the groin, and it was an Arabic arrow, and that one had ears; then I incised over it between two bones, and it was already submerged in his groin; then I dissected around it, and soon extracted it, and the Christian recovered.
And another arrow was extracted from a man which was embedded in his abdomen, and I perceived that he died of that; and therefore, after passing thirty days and no change occurring due to any cause of his condition, I incised over the arrow and managed it, and he was healed, and no ill accident befell him.
And I found a man in whom an arrow was embedded in his back, and the wound had healed over it, and after approaching seven years, incise the arrow in the crease of the thigh.
And I found a woman in whom an arrow was embedded in her abdomen, and the wound had healed inside it, and there was no change in her condition over time, and the woman did not experience any harm in any of her natural operations.
And I found another man in whom an arrow was embedded in his face, and the wound had healed, with the arrow broken inside. He did not experience much pain, and appeared similar to others.
And I extracted the arrow from the king's principal man, who was embedded in the middle of his nose, and it had slightly shifted to the right side. The entire arrow was hidden.
And I was called for the treatment of him around the arrow wound within three days.

And mostly it is found in the bodies of humid bones, such as those of children.

Capitol. iii.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not the nose itself that is broken, but two parts together with some additional cartilage above, or one of those few cracks, because in the nose there are two bones. The part you leave intact is cartilaginous and is not broken; therefore, do not damage it but apply friction and soothing.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you must introduce the small piece into the nostril, and equalize the fracture inside according to your previous indication, and outside with your thumb, so that the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this as I have facilitated for you, and make sure that the pain in the patient does not worsen due to your operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and you cannot reach it, then you must equalize it at the extremity of the ridge where there is a small swelling.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you make it appear as such.
And if the limb has restoration on it in the first day of the fracture, if possible; otherwise, as soon as the seventh or half a month, you should give it rest from the apsema caut.
And then insert into the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth wick, if the fracture is in one part, or two cloths, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the larger quantity swell to fill the nose.
And thus some of the ancient restorers say that the cloths should be soaked in broth, and changed throughout the day, but I have not seen this; instead, use a cove where the wick is soaked in honey comfit powder; and leave the cloth until the bone is firmly set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes insert into the nose instead of a cloth, a hot pack wrapped in linen cloth; and do this when the nasal fracture is severe and breathing is not prohibited.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use cloths.

And if the nose bones are broken by a small fracture or are comminuted, it is necessary to split those areas, and extract them with instruments suitable for those; then close the incision, and treat that area with what encases and consolidates, and with plasters appropriate for this.
And if a swelling occurs inside the nose, it is necessary to treat that with washes and administer lead pipes internally until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture is not displaced, consider whether the fracture is only superficial, not broken into two pieces and bent inwardly, in which case its perception is light.
And thus, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert your right index finger; and push the softness of the fracture inward with ease to the parts outward, with your other hand on the part outward of the bone; do this properly as much as that.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you should apply extension to both parts according to rightness so that the alignment of them is possible.
And if you notice the loss of teeth along with disruption, then strengthen those from which you expect the roots to be of gold or silver or cedar; and place your hand over the broken jaw, and place another hand on the doubled cloth, and place a large star-shaped stake decently made, or a single piece equal in length to the jaw; and place that above as it suits your bandage and application, so long as it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to take rest and tranquility, and stop his food intake for a while.
And if you think that the figure has already been altered in any way, on the seventh day you incise and remove it; then correct what is already altered in him and plaster the area with powder of molybdenum, or with ocher, or with flour of comfrey, provided that you have bathed his wound, and place a lion's wool bandage over the plaster.
And then, once this plaster is properly applied to him, and the position of the bone has not been altered, leave it as it is, without removing it until the fracture is healed and strong; for major fractures take three weeks to heal.
And if another time an abscess occurs, then administer what we have said about major fractures in the treatment of it until it is healed and drained.
And if the fracture is comminuted, then consider whether it is a severe bone separation or displacement. Then you should carefully handle those displacements as needed during the removal of the fragments.
And if the openings of the wound are narrow, then enlarge them according to the quantity of your necessity using the flebotomy.
And when you have completed those stages, and there is no longer any need to remain at those steps, then use the openings of the wound if they are wide; otherwise, place over it one of the plasters that suit this and cover the area until it heals.
Capitol V.

Chapter VIII.
For the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.

And already the Ancients were ingenious in that matter engaging themselves.
And from them it is said that you place the patient in a position where foods generate inflammation and distension, so that the belly becomes swollen and extended, and the fracture is driven to the outward parts.
But let us consider this so that it is not a trivial cause to generate a carbuncle inadvertently; and if it does happen inadvertently, then help is already on its way.
And some of them said that it should be positioned above the abdomen and near where sweat exits with strength; and this seems like reasoning, but nevertheless it is feared that the abdomen might drive the superfluities to the area due to weakness.
And some of them said: that the area should be covered with wool that is already submerged in oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, which should be bound equally as you wrap it according to its roundness; and then treat the patient with a regimen of food therapy and medicine.
And if the patient is strongly constrained in such a way that they cannot endure it, and you puncture the skin with a knife to drain the fluid, be careful about the patient, then cut over the place, uncovering from the broken rib, and place ourselves above that instrument which we hold ready, remembering the action beforehand, and cut the bone easily, and remove that.

Capitol. XII.
On the restoration of fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna and the radius; of which the former is small, and is that which we call the humerus, while the latter is large and is that which rests on top of the smaller one.

Chapter XIV.

Chapter XXIII.
Of speech on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of that location, rendering movement prohibited and distorting the figure of the limb, causing great pain and injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is important to immediately attempt to reduce it, and not delay the reduction process. If delayed, the area can become infected, making the reduction of the dislocation much more difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be cut or stretched during the time of infection, as severe spasms and intense pains can occur.
More specifically, when this occurs, you should prepare for an incision in the foot to drain the infection, and allow it to rest until the abscess has subsided; then, apply warm water compresses and oil to the limb, and attempt to reduce the dislocation with ease.
And each member be treated with the water of which the memory will come to its place.
And I have already and ordered the chapters of dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the part of the body first up to the part next, then leave.
Chapter 24.
For the treatment of dislocation of the jaw, proceed.
When both jaws are dislocated without a tooth; and the dislocation of both is caused according to one of two methods: either they are slightly moved from their places and made somewhat softer; or they are completely dislocated, and at the part where the jaw meets, saliva flows from the patient's mouth, so that neither can have it nor close its mouth, and the tongue of the patient is wrapped in a cloth.
More often than not, when the dislocation is slight, it returns to its place by itself with little cause.
More often than not, when the dislocation is complete and full, it must be reduced gently, and should not be delayed entirely.
And the minister hold the patient's head, and the doctor insert his thumb from one hand into the corner of the dislocated jaw inside the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or he inserts both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands remain outside and equalize; and command the patient to cease moving his jaw and allow those to return from any part; and the doctor equalize the jaw and push it back into its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are completely dislocated, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil, while performing a light reduction of both and do not delay the reduction of both entirely, as we have said before.
And when returned and equalized, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not softened, then place it on powders of cloth with cerate, made of wax and rubbed oil; and apply it with several soft cloths.
And let the patient sleep on their back, with their head standing between two pillows, so that it does not get wet on either side; do not force them to gargle for any reason; having their food ready by a lion skin, when the pains subside and their jaws are moistened, then wet what they want.
And this should be given easily, and you must not work on the aperture of the mouth; let them eat, drink, and breathe freely, when their jaw is healed.
And if the reduction of two jaws when dislocated at any time and cannot be returned to their proper places, then blisters often occur, causing frequent fever and a constant dryness. The belly of the patient is also strongly swollen, with severe vomiting of pure bile.
And when you see this, then know it is fatal; and blisters often occur to those upon whom this happens in a few days.

Chapter XXVI.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated in any manner other than three ways, of which one is when it is dislocated from the tendon to the bone.
And the second is when it is dislocated above the muscle, which is called tart; and it is not dislocated at the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated outward at the lower parts of the tendon, especially in those where the muscles are few, it is easily dislocated; more so in those where there are many muscles, it occurs with difficulty.
And often such persons suffer from a blow or strike, and the muscle of the person develops an abscess due to a boil, and becomes inflamed because it is already dislocated.
Then cover what you are especially over them with, and then proceed in the cure of them.

When you palpate the head of the muscle, it feels like a hollow space, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor can he move it with all movements.
If it is dislocated, you must palpate the foot or upwards, and then you will find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.

And if you wish, return the second in this manner: and it is that you make a person present who is longer than the patient, standing on the side of that lathe, and insert his muscle under the titillate of the patient, elevating the titillate above the parts above while the patient hangs in the air, and another minister pulls the hand of the patient from the parts below of his abdomen. And extend the hand on both sides of the patient; and stretch the body of the patient with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.

And place cloths on the round head of the pole so that the pole does not press against the patient.

Chapter XXVII.
On the cure of dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder joint is dislocated with difficulty and seems to return with difficulty; it can be dislocated in all directions, but most commonly in front and behind. The dislocation of the shoulder is not hidden; whether you observe it quickly or feel it by touch, depending on the shape of the displacement. When you reduce the dislocated shoulder into place, this is clearly evident to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm or reach its own muscle.
And note that if an apostema (abscess) has formed, reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it reaches the apostema. For if it does reach the apostema, the reduction becomes difficult, and even with strong measures, it may not be completely healed. Especially when it is in the rear parts, for this type of dislocation is worse than all others, causing more pain and often leading to death if not treated promptly.
And the restoration of it, when that is possible to reduce, is that his hand be extended between two others, and his arm be stretched out, and the hands of the doctor be on the knees and leave him, and he push the joint with the heels of his hands or with the edge of his palm as it returns to its place.
And if the dislocation is in the front parts, then return it thus by bending his hand towards his own hand, striking the knee with the edge of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer strong extension and vigorous movement.
And have two ministers stretch out their arms, and hold that position with two ministers so that the extension is not disturbed.
And then rotate the arm completely around, since it is wrapped in a long folded cloth around the patient's hand, or bandage.
